iii
Abstract
This thesis studies the application of Operational Modal Analysis on a flexible rotor-bearing
system through experimental work.
The Operational Modal Analysis techniques used are Ibrahim Time Domain (ITD), Eigen-
system Realisation Algorithm (ERA), Data-Driven Stochastic Subspace Identification (SSI),
and Enhanced Frequency Domain Decomposition (FDD).
The system consists of a shaft, a disc, an active gas bearing with a piezo-actuated injection
system, a self-aligning ball bearing, and an electric motor rotating the rotor.
The piezo
actuators are used to excite the system with white noise.
The main objective of this thesis is to investigate the possibility of tracking the natural
frequency and damping ratio change of the forward mode depending on the parameters:
angular velocity, injection pressure, valve configuration, excitation amplitude, and the number
of valves exciting the system. The experiments follow a 2k factorial experimental design, where
each parameter was designated a low and a high level.
To verify the accuracy of the Operational Modal Analysis techniques, a series of Experimental
Modal Analysis experiments are conducted for comparison.
The results obtained from the experimental work show that it is possible to track the natural
frequency and damping ratio changes depending on the system’s parameters.
The FDD
technique is the most accurate of the techniques and results, on average, in the best estimates
for the natural frequencies and damping ratios, followed by SSI and ERA. The ITD technique
is inadequate at low pressures and excitation amplitudes.

==PAGE:11==
1
Introduction
1.1
Gas Bearings and Modal Analysis in Industry
Gas bearings are becoming increasingly popular in the industry due to their many advantages.
The main advantages are the low friction between the rotating components and high angular
velocity.
Another advantage is that they can be used in clean environments due to not
using lubricating oil or grease. Furthermore, their operating cost is significantly reduced if
atmospheric air is used as the lubricating gas.
Multiple challenges arise when using gas bearings.
The main disadvantages are the low
stability threshold and low load-carrying capacity. These disadvantages result from the gas’s
low viscosity, and the damping properties are similarly low due to the low viscosity. The low
damping of the systems leads to high vibration amplitudes when approaching and crossing
critical speeds. The high amplitudes lead to contact between the bearing walls and the rotor,
damaging or destroying the bearing. The use of active regulation can overcome this. Active
regulation can be used for controlling the injection of the pressurised gas into the bearing with
the use of piezo actuators. Controlling the injection of air alters the dynamic characteristics
of the rotor bearing system and can hence be used for limiting the vibration amplitudes.
For active regulation, the dynamic characteristics of the system has to be known. For esti-
mating, these characteristics modal analyses can be used.
The modal analysis estimates the dynamic characteristics of the structure under test. These
characteristics are used not only for active regulation of the system but also for many other
applications, such as estimating structural integrity, vibration locations used for comfort, and
the reduction of noise.
In Experimental Modal Analysis, EMA, the structure is impacted under test, and the input
and output signals are measured.
These signals are the basis for the estimations of the
system’s natural frequencies, damping ratios, and mode shapes. This technique is used in all
fields of mechanical engineering, from aerospace and defence to the windmill industry.
Operational Modal Analysis, OMA, uses only the output signal as the basis for estimation of
the system’s natural frequencies, damping ratios, and mode shapes. This is done by assuming
that the input signal of the system is random environmental noise, i.e. white noise. This
technique is primarily used for structures where it is impractical or impossible to excite the
system by impacts. These structures are mainly in the construction industry. Some examples
of structures could be buildings, bridges, dams, and offshore platforms. Due to its low test
cost, OMA is also primarily used for structural health monitoring of the structures.
1.2
State of the Art at DTU
Bearings with different designs have been tested and researched at DTU for many years. The
test rig used for this thesis was constructed in 2008 [1] for research on passive-magnetic bump
foil bearings. The test rig was altered in 2010 [2] when the controllable active gas bearing used
for this thesis was installed. Four piezo actuators were installed to control the gas bearing, 90
degrees shifted from each other. The magnetic bearing was removed, and the active control
was studied in 2016 [3].
OMA Applied to Rotor-Bearing Systems via Pneumatic Actuation - Low Damping and Air
1
==PAGE:12==
2
1.3. The Aim of this Thesis
In 2019 the hybrid gas bearing was constructed [4], and its implementation was attempted,
but due to practical issues, the implementation was not done, and the active bearing was
reinstalled.
In 2022 the test rig was improved, [5], where the gas-driven motor was replaced with an
electrical motor, and the electrical system of the test rig was updated due to safety concerns.
The implementation of the electrical motor reduced vibration noise, improved the stability of
the angular velocity, and simplified the control of the test rig.
1.3
The Aim of this Thesis
The goal of this master thesis is to investigate the possibility of applying OMA techniques
and their corresponding computational tools for signal processing in rotor dynamics. OMA
techniques are applied to track the changes in modal parameters depending on the rotor’s
angular velocity, injection pressure, and valve configurations. The gas bearing’s active control
will be used to excite the system with white noise, and the horizontal and vertical displacement
will be measured. The OMA techniques use the displacements to determine the system’s
natural frequencies and damping ratios. Furthermore, the significant effect that influence
the estimations of the OMA techniques will be investigated. To verify the accuracy of the
OMA techniques, EMA is utilised. This is done since EMA uses the input force and the
output displacement to determine modal parameters in contrast to OMA, which only uses
the output, making EMA the more precise of the methods. OMA and EMA are compared
using several statistical tools.
The thesis is structured the following way:
Chapter 2: Provides a background on the theory behind OMA and EMA and a brief intro-
duction to the statistical tools used for this thesis.
Chapter 3: Test rig setup with its mechanical and electronic components is introduced, and
the procedure for calibrating and aligning the test rig is formalised.
Chapter 4: The test parameters are chosen, the experimental test plan is constructed, and
the OMA and EMA experimental procedures are introduced. Furthermore, the challenges
and limitations of the test rig are described.
Chapter 5: The results are presented and discussed.
Chapter 6: The conclusion and reflection on the results are presented.
==PAGE:13==
2
Theoretical Foundation
This chapter describes the theory of the different techniques and methods used in this thesis.
These include the mathematical methods used in this thesis’s two types of modal analysis.
These methods include, among others: the calculations of correlation function, two types
of decomposition of matrices, the calculations of spectral densities, transfer functions, least
square fits and the theory behind the modal analysis types. The last part of this chapter is
dedicated to introducing the statistical tool ued for the interpretation of the modal parameter
estimates.
The two modal analysis types are Operational Modal Analysis, known as OMA, and Experi-
mental Modal Analysis, known as EMA. In OMA, four different methods are used, three in
the time domain and one in the frequency domain.
The basis for the OMA theory and techniques are supplied by [6]. The framework used for the
MATLAB code developed for OMA is supplied by [6] and is adapted, altered, and improved
during this thesis to obtain the modal parameters for the test rig described in chapter 3.
The basis for the EMA theory is supplied by [7] and [8]. The foundation for the MATLAB
code for the modal analysis of the obtained data was developed by Ilmar Santos and expanded
by Simon Friborg Mortensen for his master thesis in the spring of 2022 [5]. This code was
modified for the data processing of this thesis.
The statistical theory is supplied by [9] and [10].
The theory presented in this chapter is briefly described, and only the most essential elements
and equations are shown. For a more in-depth description, the reader is referred to OMA:
[6], EMA: [7] and [8], and [9] and [10] for the statistical theory.
2.1
Operational Modal Analysis, OMA, Theory
The OMA methods used in this thesis are:
Time Domain Methods
• Ibrahim Time Domain, ITD
• Eigensystem Realisation Algorithm, ERA
• Data-Driven Stochastic Subspace Identification, SSI
Frequency Domain Methods
• Enhanced Frequency Domain Decomposition, FDD
ITD and ERA are chosen since they are easy to implement and yield good results in many
practical cases. SSI and FDD are chosen based on yielding good results and widespread use
in the industry.
OMA Applied to Rotor-Bearing Systems via Pneumatic Actuation - Low Damping and Air
3
==PAGE:14==
4
2.1. Operational Modal Analysis, OMA, Theory
2.1.1
Assumptions
For a qualitative estimation of the modal parameters, certain assumptions have to be made:
• The excitation of the system is Gaussian distributed.
• The correlation function extracts all, or nearly all, information about the modal char-
acteristics of the system from the Gaussian distributed excitation.
• The correlation function can be considered as a free decay due to suﬀiciently light
damping.
• The system is linear and time-invariant.
• The response is ergodic and, therefore, also stationary.
The most crucial assumption in OMA is that the system’s input is Gaussian/Normal dis-
tributed, exciting all frequencies of the system over the whole structure. From a more prac-
tical point of view, the Central Limit Theorem states that a sum of many independent and
identically distributed random variables will tend to be Gaussian distributed as the number
of variables approaches infinity. Furthermore, the signal will approach Gaussian distribution
regardless of the type of distribution of the individual random variables in the linear combi-
nation. The significant implication of the Gaussian input is that the Gaussian distribution
has zero average, and thus, all the signals’ information is in the signal’s second-order proper-
ties. This implies that the correlation function describes all information on the signals. Note
that all information about the signals can also be found in the spectral density. The spectral
density is used for identification in the frequency domain. Once the correlation function has
decayed, all information about the system is contained within the non-decayed correlation
function; this is further discussed in Section 2.1.8.
Assuming that the system is linear implies that the input and output are regarded as linear
functions. Hence introducing a constant on the input will result in the same constant on the
output. The assumption that the system is invariant assumes that the system’s output would
be the same regardless of the time the input/excitation was applied to the system.
The assumption that the system is ergodic implies that the system will visit all parts of the
space the system can occupy. Furthermore, it implies that the system can move uniformly
and randomly. This results in the average of an ergodic signal, and the mean of the signal can
be substituted by the time average of the signal. When a system is stationary, the Probability
Density Function, PDF, is not a function of times and hence does not change with time. In
practice, most systems are invariant or close to it, and the lack of stationarity is mainly due
to a change in loading conditions. Even though, the assumption of stationarity is a premise
for using OMA; in practice, reliable results can be obtained under weakly non-stationary
conditions.
The correlation function’s length depends on the size of the damping factor.
The more
significant the damping factor, the faster the correlation function decays, decreasing the
fundament for estimation and thus increasing the error of the estimation.
if closely spaced modes are present they have to be geometrically orthogonal to have any
physical meaning.
==PAGE:15==
Chapter 2. Theoretical Foundation

2.1.3
Correlation
The correlation describes the similarity of a time series with itself, known as the auto-
correlation, or with other signals, known as the cross-correlation. This implies that a com-
pletely random signal would have theoretical, zero correlation, and two time series with
identical amplitude at all times would be perfectly correlated.
The auto-correlation and
cross-correlation are further described below.
Auto-correlation
The auto-correlation describes the relationship between the current signal value at x(t) and
the signal at x(t + τ) where τ is the time lag, the separation between the signals.
The
auto-correlation is defined as [6, eq. (2.27)]:
Rx(τ) = E[x(t)x(t + τ)]
(2.5)
Since the signal has a Gaussian distribution, the mean of the signal will approach zero for
large time lags. Further, since the signal is assumed stationary, the auto-correlation function
is independent of time, and any time shift introduced will not change the results.
This
leads to the realisation that Rx(τ) = Rx(−τ) hence resulting in a symmetry relation around
τ = 0. Note: when τ = 0, the correlation function equals the variance σ2
xx. In practice, the
auto-correlation is obtained using time averaging, equation (2.2) [6, eq. (2.30)]:
Rx(τ) = 1
T
Z T
0
x(t)x(t + τ)dt
(2.6)
==PAGE:16==
6
2.1. Operational Modal Analysis, OMA, Theory
Cross-correlation
The cross-correlation describes the relation between two signals as a function of the time shift
between the signals. The cross-correlation is calculated by generalising equation (2.5) into
two random signals [6, eq. (2.31)]:
Rxy(τ)
= E[x(t)y(t + τ)] = E[x(t)y(t −τ)]
Ryx(τ)
= E[y(t)x(t + τ)] = E[y(t)x(t −τ)]
(2.7)

Rxy(τ) = 1

2.1.5
Singular Value Decomposition
The Singular Value Decomposition, SVD, of a matrix is a factorisation of the matrix into
three matrices. The SVD of a general matrix A can be expressed as [6, eq. (3.56)]:
A = USVH
(2.14)
where S is a diagonal matrix holding real and non-negative singular values of A. These are
in descending order, with the largest value in the upper left corner. U and V are unitary
matrices holding the columns vectors called the singular vectors. These matrices are defined
as: [6, eq. (3.57), (3.58)]:
S = [sn],
U = [un],
and
V = [vn]
(2.15)
Consider an SVD where S is square and the number of vectors in U and V are equal. Then
the number of rows in A will be given by the number of components in the singular vectors
un. The number of columns in A will be given by the number of components in the singular
vectors vn. By multiplying the matrices in equation (2.14), the equation leads to a series of
outer products:
A =
R
X

srurvH
R
(2.16)
where R is given by the number of nonzero singular values.
2.1.6
QR Decomposition
The QR decomposition or QR Factorisation of a matrix is a decomposition of a matrix A
into an orthogonal matrix Q and an upper triangular matrix R, i.e.
A = QR
(2.17)
There are several methods for computing the QR decomposition. The method used in this
thesis is the Gram-Schmidt process. The orthogonal matrix Q of a matrix A = [a1, a2, ..., an]
is found by calculating the orthonormal basis, v = [v1, v2, ..., vn], and the new basis vectors,

2.1.8
Time Domain Identification Techniques
The primary tool for calculating time domain identification is the correlation function. The
idea of using the correlation function is that, as described in Section 2.1.1, all the information
about the system is captured using the correlation function.
If a Gaussian distributed force is added to the system, the system will oscillate as a function
of the random input. The system response will not be random since the Gaussian distributed
force will excite all natural frequencies and modes. Hence the system will tend to oscillate at
its natural frequencies. By analysing the frequencies and decay of the correlation function,
the system’s natural frequencies and damping ratios can be determined.
The most fundamental steps using time domain OMA can be described as follows:
1. Obtaining a response.
2. Calculate the correlation function from the response and apply a window to cut off the
noise tail. This is done since all information about the system is in the non-decayed
part of the correlation function. Removing the noise tail results in less noise in the
further estimations. The removal of the noise tail is done by limiting the number of
time lags in the correlation function.
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2. Theoretical Foundation
9
3. The correlation function is used as an OMA time domain technique input. This is
not the case with the Stochastic Subspace Identification technique since the correlation
function estimation is an integrated part of the method. The OMA technique then tries
to make a fit to the correlation function.
4. The OMA techniques estimate the correlation function, eigenvalues and eigenvectors.
The correlation function can be used to compare with the correlation function inputted
into the method; if a slight difference is observed the estimation is deemed acceptable.
The eigenvalues are used to calculate the natural frequencies and damping ratios, and
the eigenvectors are the mode shapes.
The individual methods used in this thesis are now introduced.
Ibrahim Time Domain (ITD)
The Ibrahim Time Domain assumes that any free response, for example, a correlation function
matrix, can be formulated as a linear combination of mode shapes and exponential decays [6,
eq. (9.30)]:
y(t) = y (k∆t) = c1a1e(λ1k∆t) + c2a2e(λ2k∆t) + · · · = c1a1µk

2 + · · ·
(2.22)

continuous time poles and µ1, µ2, ... are discrete-time poles. The block Hankel matrix in the
ITD technique is defined with four block rows [6, eq. (9.32)]:
H =

and the discrete-time poles are determined by taking the square root of the diagonal elements

Using eigenvalue decomposition there are as many eigenvalues as the dimension of the matrix
bA. The size of the system matrix bA is 2nc × 2nc, where nc is the number of channels in the
correlation function. There are hence 2nc number of eigenvalues and nc number of modes.

(np −3)nr >> 2nc
(2.27)
where nr is the number of correlation functions used in the technique.

Data-Driven Stochastic Subspace Identification (DD-SSI)
There are two main types of Stochastic Subspace Identification techniques, the Covariance
Driven Stochastic Subspace Identification method, Cov-SSI, and the Data-Driven Stochastic
Subspace Identification, DD-SSI method. The latter is the one that will be discussed further
since the Cov-SSI method is very similar to the ERA technique. The DD-SSI, now only
referred to as SSI, differentiates itself from the previously discussed techniques, ITD and
ERA, due to the direct input of the measured response into the block Hankel matrix [6, eq.
(9.66)]:

The matrix is split into two equal size blocks, the upper matrix, H1, is typically called
the past and the lower matrix, H2, is typically called the future. The past block Hankel
matrix is treated as deterministic conditioned, and the future block Hankel matrix is treated
==PAGE:22==
12
2.1. Operational Modal Analysis, OMA, Theory
as stochastic.
The past and future block Hankel matrices are then used to calculate the
projection matrix, O [6, eq. (9.67)-(9.69)]:
O = E[H2|H1] = T21T+
11H1 = H2HT
1 (H1HT
1 )+H1
(2.40)
It should be noted that the product of the first four terms produces the covariance for the data
channels at different time lags. Furthermore, the last term, H1, defines the initial conditions.
Since it is assumed that the signals are Gaussian distributed, the columns of O are stacked free
decays to a set of unknown initial conditions, hence being equivalent to a correlation function.
It should also be noted that there is a close similarity between equation (2.40) and (2.25).
Hence the solution to equation (2.40) can be calculated relatively easily. However, since the
block Hankel matrix, equation (2.39), is created using the signal data, it is significantly larger
than the block Hankel matrices in equation (2.25), hence being computationally expensive.
To avoid these expensive calculations the projection matrix is found by using QR decomposi-
tion of the transposed block Hankel matrix. From this, an orthogonal matrix Q and an upper
triangular matrix R are obtained. Similar to equation (2.34) the projection matrix can be
expressed as [6, eq. (9.70)]:
O = ΓX
(2.41)
where the observability matrix, Γ, is the same as shown in equation (2.31) and X is the initial

decomposition is taken of equation (2.41), resulting in:
O = USVT
(2.42)

corresponding to 1
2n modes. The matrices U and V are likewise restricted to be of the same

observability matrix and the initial condition matrix X can be expressed as [6, eq. (9.72)]:
bΓ = Un

bΓ(2:s)bD = bΓ(1:s−1)
(2.44)

2.1.9
Frequency Domain Identification (FDI)
Another method of using operational modal analysis is by using Frequency Domain Identi-
fication techniques, where most of the identification technique takes place in the frequency
domain. In these techniques, the primary tool is the spectral densities. These identification
schemes have some advantages and disadvantages. There are two main advantages. First
advantage: The techniques are usually more user-friendly since the user can see and select
frequencies of interest.
Second advantage: estimating the modal parameters is simplified
by assuming that each mode has a small frequency band where they dominate. Hence it is
possible to estimate the parameters based on a small band for the mode of interest. The
disadvantage of assuming modes dominates in small bands is that the mode still contributes
to the entire frequency band, hence some information is lost, and it is hard to obtain bias-free
estimations. The spectral densities are found by the Fourier transforming the correlation
function as stated in Section 2.1.7
The most fundamental steps for using frequency domain OMA can be described as follows:
1. Obtaining a response.
2. Calculate the correlation function and, after that, the spectral densities. As with the
time domain applying a window to cut the noise tail can be advantageous. The spectral
densities are then plotted as a function of frequency, for the determination of the width
and peak of each mode of interest.
3. The spectral density function is used as an OMA frequency domain technique input.
The OMA method then tries to make a fit to the spectral density function.
4. The spectral density is then transformed back into the time domain using inverse Fourier
transformation, creating the estimated correlation function.
5. The OMA method outputs the estimation of the fitted correlation function. The cor-
relation function can be compared with the correlation function inputted into the algo-
rithm; if a slight difference is observed, then the estimation is deemed acceptable. The
eigenvalues and eigenvectors, mode shapes, are then transformed into physical modal
parameters and are used to calculate the natural frequencies and damping ratios.
This project’s only frequency domain technique is the Frequency Domain Decomposition
technique.
Frequency Domain Decomposition (FDD)
The Frequency Domain Decomposition technique, FDD, assumes that the response can be
expressed as a summation of normal modes and modal coordinates.[6, eq. (10.13)]
y(t) = a1q1(t) + a2q2(t) + ... = Aq(t)
(2.48)
where A = [a1, a1, ...] is the mode shape matrix and q(t) = {q1(t), q2(t), ...}T is a vector of
modal coordinates. Using the definition of cross-correlation function from equation (2.9), the
cross-correlation function can be expressed as [6, eq. (10.14)]:
Ry(τ) = E

y(t)yT(t + τ)

= AE

q(t)qT(t + τ)

AT = ARq(τ)AT
(2.49)
where Rq(τ) is the correlation function matrix of modal coordinates.
The corresponding
spectral density matrix can be found by Fourier transformation of the correlation function:
Gy(f) = AGq(f)AT
(2.50)
==PAGE:24==
14
2.1. Operational Modal Analysis, OMA, Theory
Assuming that the modal coordinates are uncorrelated yields that the off-diagonal elements in
Rq(τ) are zero, simplify Gq(f) to a positive diagonal matrix. This is due to the auto-spectral
density function always being positive. Since the spectral density matrix is known to be
Hermitian and that complexity might be present in the mode shapes, if damping is present,
then equation (2.50) can be written as [6, eq. (10.16)]:
Gy(f) = A

g2
n(f)

AH
(2.51)
where g2
n(f) are the auto-spectral-densities of Gy(f). The decomposition of equation (2.51)
can now be done using SVD and since Gy(f) is complex, Hermitian and positive definite the
approximation of the decomposition becomes [6, eq. (10.17)]:
Gy(f) = USUH = U

s2
n

UH
(2.52)
It follows that the singular values s2
n in the diagonal matrix S can be interpreted as the auto-
spectral densities, [s2

n(f)]. The singular vectors U = [u1, u2, ...] can be interpreted as
the mode shapes.
The equations from (2.48) to (2.52) are the core of the FDD method.
The FDD is a peak-picking approach, the user picks the peaks of interest, and then a rough
estimation of the natural frequency and mode shape can be obtained directly from the peaks.
The quality of the estimation is limited by the resolution and the noise of the spectral density.
Furthermore, the estimation of equation (2.52) does not provide an estimation for the damping
in the system.
Since a modal parameter is missing, and to improve the estimation of the natural frequencies,
the Enhanced Frequency Domain Decomposition, EFDD, is introduced. The concept of this
technique is to include the information around the peak for the estimation.
Then using,
inverse Fourier transformation to transform it back to the time domain, i.e. obtaining the
auto-correlation function for that specific mode. Then a time domain technique is used to
obtain the natural frequencies and damping. The main flaw of this technique is that it has
diﬀiculties when handling closely separated modes, hence modal filtering is introduced to
isolate the modal coordinates. This is introduced by defining a matrix V = [v1, v2, ...] such
that [6, eq. (10.30)]:
VHA = I
(2.53)
As can be seen from equation (2.53), V can be defined as the Hermitian of the pseudo inverse
of the mode shape matrix: i.e. V = (A+)H. Hence the vectors in V are orthogonal to the
mode shapes in A. This yields that when an inner product of any vectors from the orthogonal
set for the spectral density matrix isolates the spectral densities of the modal coordinates:
vH
n Gy(f)vn = g2
n(f)
(2.54)
With the filtration implemented, the spectral density is then transformed back into the time
domain using the inverse fast Fourier transformation, where an estimation of higher precision
of the natural frequency and damping can be conducted. The time domain technique used in
this thesis is the ITD technique.

2.2
Experimental Modal Analysis, EMA, Theory
In Experimental Modal Analysis, EMA, the input and output of the system are measured.
The input used in this project is a force impulse from an impact hammer. A transfer function
can be constructed from the input and output by dividing the output with the input; this
transfer function is called the Frequency Response Function, FRF. The FRF is obtained by
using the auto-spectral density and the cross-spectral density of the force input x(t) and
the output displacement y(t), calculated as in Section 2.1.7, to create two complex transfer
functions:
H1(ω) = Gxy(ω)
Gx(ω)
and
H2(ω) = Gy(ω)
Gyx(ω)
(2.55)
H1(ω) is better at estimating anti-resonances and H2(ω) is better at estimating resonances.
This is due to the auto-spectral density of the force being used in H1(ω). The force is typically
low at resonances, resulting in noise being more significant. H2(ω) is better at estimating
resonances due to the displacement being used. Displacements are typically high at resonances
and low at anti-resonances, resulting in noise being more significant at anti-resonances.
To quantify the quality of the results, the coherence function, γ2, is calculated. This function
calculates the ratio between the input and output as follows:
γ2 =
|Gyx(ω)|2
Gy(ω)Gx(ω) = H1(ω)
H2(ω)
(2.56)
If the coherence is 1, then there is complete consistency between H1(ω) and H2(ω); hence no
noise is present in the signals. Thus coherence is a measurement of how contaminated the
signals are; high coherence reflects good results.
The phase ϕ is also a critical parameter in the EMA technique since it shows phase shifts,
and how the output and input move relative to each other. The phase is calculated by the
inverse cosine function between the real and value and the modulus:
ϕ = arccos
Re (H(ω))
|H(ω)|

(2.57)
This is done since the transfer functions are complex.
To obtain the modal parameters from the FRF, a Least Square Fit around the peaks of the
FRF is performed. For performing a least square fit, it is necessary to assume that the mode
being analysed is dominant in the frequency range around the peak and that the mode can be
described as a Single Degree of Freedom System, SDOF. The theoretical FRF for an SDOF
is defined as [7, eq. (2.60)]:
H(jω) =
1

(2.58)
where k is the stiffness, m is the mass, and d is the damping. This is split into a real and
imaginary part with n number of natural frequencies. An error vector attached to both the

2.3
Statistical Theory
This section presents a short introduction to the statistical theory used in this thesis.
2.3.1
Confidence Interval
Estimating the mean of a sample is not usually enough since, in most scenarios, the analyst
wants to know how close the sample mean is to the population mean, the true mean. This
quantification of the uncertainty is the confidence interval, CI. For determining the confidence
interval for the population mean, the population is assumed to be Gaussian distributed and
hence the (1 −α)% confidence interval can be determined by [9, Method 3.9]
x ± t1−α/2
s
√n
(2.63)
where x is the mean of the sample, t1−α/2 is the 1 −α quantile from a t-distribution with
n −1 degrees of freedom, n being the number of observations. s is the standard deviation for
one sample and is determined by [9, eq. (1.11)]:
s =
v
u
u
t
1
n −1
n
X
i=1
(xi −x)2
(2.64)
Note: when calculating the confidence interval for multiple samples, x and s are capitalised,
and n becomes the number of samples.
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 2. Theoretical Foundation
17
2.3.2
2k Factorial Design
The experimental design chosen for this thesis is a 2k factorial design. All theory regarding
the 2k factorial design is provided by [10]. This design is due to its wide use in research
work and provides the smallest number of runs with which the k factors1 can be studied as
a complete factorial design. Furthermore, it is normally used in screening experiments where
the experiments are used to find the active factors in a large set of factors. The k factors
each have two levels, a low and a high level. These levels can be quantitative or qualitative,
depending on the type of experiments. For the 2k factorial design to work, some assumptions
must be made.
1. The factors are fixed; the factors do not change during individual tests.
2. The tests are completely randomised; this is done to average out the extraneous factors
that may be present and could introduce biases in the results.
3. Normality of the distribution of errors is assumed.
4. Since only two levels are used for each factor, it is assumed that the response is approxi-
mately linear between the two levels chosen.
An obvious risk when performing experiments is that the estimated effect of a factor is
erroneously found to be almost zero due to the levels of that factor not being separated
enough compared to the variability present in the response variable. Hence to reduce the
likelihood of an erroneous conclusion the levels of the factors are attempted to be adequate
spaces; an example of this can be seen in Figure 2.1.
Figure 2.1: The impact of the choice of factor levels, in figure A, the factor levels are inade-
quately spaced compared to figure B, where they are adequately spaced.
This is rarely a problem when replicating experiments, but it should still be considered.
An experimental plan has to be created for conducting the experiments of a 2k factorial
design. This is done by creating a design matrix consisting of k factors, and 2k runs in a
unique pattern where no factor combinations are repeated. For a 22 factorial design, see
Table 2.1. When this is created, the number of replications is determined, and the table is
repeated the number of replications in the vertical direction. The table is then randomised
to average out the extraneous factors. The k parameters chosen are, by convention, denoted
as capital Latin letters A, B and so forth.
1Factors and parameters describe the same, in this thesis
==PAGE:28==
18
2.3. Statistical Theory

2.3.4
Normal Probability Plot
The normal probability plot is a graphical technique for determining if the sample data
conform to a hypothesised distribution. For constructing a probability plot, the sample data,
y1, y2, ..., yn is arranged from smallest to largest such that y(1), y(2), ..., y(j), ..., y(n) with y(1)
being the smallest and y(n) being the largest. The j observation, y(j) is then plotted against
the observed cumulative frequency, (j −0.5)/n. This frequency is arranged such that if the
data conform to the hypothesised distribution, they fall along a straight line. Usually, a
straight line is drawn through the plot from the 25th to the 75th percentile points. The
distribution is confirmed if the arranged data falls approximately along this straight line.
This technique is used in Section 5.2 in Figure 5.11.
2.3.5
ANOVA
The Analysis of Variance, ANOVA, tests the hypotheses about the main effect and interactions
for a fixed effect model. The hypothesis is generally that no effects, τ, influence the mean
on the treatment means. This is called the null hypothesis H0. The case where the null
hypothesis is not true is called the alternative hypothesis, H1:
H0 : τ1 = τ2 = ... = τa
(2.66)
H1 : τi ̸= 0 for at least one i
(2.67)
Hence the ANOVA tests if the treatment effects are zero, therefore an analysis of variance.
The main objective of the ANOVA testing is to obtain the ratio between the treatment mean
sum of squares and the mean sum of errors, F0 [10, eq. (3.7)]:
F0 = MSTreatment
MSE
(2.68)
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 2. Theoretical Foundation
19
MSE is an unbiased estimator of the variance σ2, E(MSE) = σ2 and if the null hypothesis H0
is true MSTreatment is an unbiased estimator of σ2, hence resulting in F0 = 1. Therefore under
the alternative hypothesis H1, F0 becomes larger than one and the hypothesis H0 should be
rejected if F0 becomes too large. Typically to determine when F0 is too large, the p-value
approach is used, where a significance level is chosen. The p-value is the probability that
the test statistic will take on a value that is at least as extreme as the observed value of
the statistic when the null hypothesis is true. Hence the p-value defines the smallest level of
significance that would lead to the rejection of the null hypothesis.
The interpretations of the p-values are typical:
p < 0.001
Very strong evidence against H0
0.001 ≤p < 0.01
Strong evidence against H0
0.01 ≤p < 0.05
Some evidence against H0
0.05 ≤p < 0.1
Weak evidence against H0
p ≥0.1
Little or no evidence against H0
Table 2.3: Interpretation of the p-value [9, Tab. 3.1]

Test Rig Setup
This chapter describes the test rig used for this thesis.
The test rig setup, as seen from the front in Figure 3.1 and the back in Figure 3.2, is located
in the mechanical test hall at DTU Lyngby, building 414. The test rig consists of a flexible
shaft, I, with an overhung steel disc, II. The rotor system is supported by a self-aligning ball
bearing (SKF 1200 ETN9), III, and an active gas bearing, IV. The gas bearing is not used
for active control of the system in this project, but the injection nozzles are used to excite
the system.
An electrical motor, V, is connected to the rotor system by a flexible coupling, VI, and is
controlled by a Danfoss VLT Micro Drive, VII. Except for the VLT, the entire test rig is
mounted to a 20mm thick aluminium plate, VIII, which in turn is mounted to a steel testing
frame IX, with a layer of rubber between the plate and the stand to avoid transferring vibra-
tions between the two. The active gas bearing is supplied with air from an external pressure
tank, pressurised to approximately 7.5bar. The tank is located in the basement of building
414. The test rig is controlled by a computer using the program dSPACE ControlDesk, where
the motor’s rotational speed and the gas bearing can be controlled.
Figure 3.1: The test rig setup from the front.
OMA Applied to Rotor-Bearing Systems via Pneumatic Actuation - Low Damping and Air
21
==PAGE:32==
22
3.1. Mechanical Setup
Figure 3.2: The test rig setup from the back
3.1
Mechanical Setup
The rotor bearing system consists of a 500mm flexible shaft made of stainless steel AISI 314,
with a mass of 2.04kg. Where the shaft passes within the gas-bearing bushing, a sleeved of
34CrNiMo6 is shrink-fitted to the shaft. The material is heat treated to harden the surface.
This reduces wear in case of contact between the shaft and bushing. Mounted to the shaft is
a ferromagnetic carbon steel disc with a mass of 1.5kg and a diameter of 139mm. The back
side of the disc is fitted with 16 threaded holes in pairs of 4, 90 degrees from each other.
These are used for inserting small masses to balance the rotor system. The rotor system can
be seen with dimensions in Figure 3.3.
Figure 3.3: The rotor system and the flexible shaft, with dimensions in mm.

3.1.1
The Active Gas Bearing
The gas active gas bearing seen in Figure 3.4 consists of five main components. A bronze
bearing bushing IV.1, an aluminium bearing house IV.2, four air injection systems IV.3, two
adjustable aluminium mounts IV.4, and an aluminium connection plate connecting the gas
bearing system with the test rig. Furthermore, the flexible shaft sleeve can be seen marked
with IV.6 and the horizontal displacement sensor marked with IV.7.
Figure 3.4: The active gas bearing
The bearing house IV.2 is used to mount the bearing bushing and the air injection system; the
bearing house is, in turn, mounted to the adjustable aluminium mounts and the connection
plate to connect the bearing system to the test rig.
The active gas bearing supports the rotor system by creating a high-pressure air film from
the injected air. The air is injected from four nozzles; these are placed 90 degrees from each
other, two in the vertical plane and two in the horizontal plane, as seen in Figure 3.5. The
injectors are numbered as seen in Figure 3.5 and will be referred to these numbers throughout
this thesis.
==PAGE:34==
24
3.1. Mechanical Setup
The airflow in the nozzles is controlled by piezo actua-
tors, enabling the possibility of active control. The air
supply for each injector can be shut off manually by
valves enabling the possibility of not using the hori-
zontal injectors for some of the tests. The air pressure
for the injectors is controlled by a pressure regulator
(Festo MS4-LFR-1/8-D7-CRM-AS) and the pressure
is measured live during the tests by a transducer (Dan-
foss Type AKS 32).
The total airflow through the
bearing is measured by a flow meter (Festo SFAB).
The active gas bearing can be used for the active con-
trol of the bearing, however, is out of the scope of the
thesis.
The bronze sleeve is designed to easily deform and
absorb the impact of the shaft if there is contact be-
tween the two. The bearing has a diameter of 40mm, a
length of 40mm, a nominal clearing of 35.1µm found in
Section 5.1, and an orifice radius 1mm for each nozzle.

3.1.2
The Flexible Coupling
Connecting the motor to the rotor-bearing system is a flexible coupling consisting of a rubber
hose; this can be seen in Figure 3.6. The hose is approximately 50mm long, has a diameter
of 10mm, and a wall thickness of 1.5mm. The purpose of the flexible coupling is to minimise
the travel of vibrations from the motor to the rotor system, which would otherwise interfere
with displacement measurements.
Figure 3.6: The flexible coupling connecting the motor to the rotor system.
==PAGE:35==
Chapter 3. Test Rig Setup
25
One disadvantage of using a rubber hose is that the hose can only be connected to the rotor and
motor by an expansion fit. The result is that the hose is deformed and, due to creep reduced
the connection pressure over time, increasing the risk of detaching during experimental work.
Another disadvantage is that using a rubber hose induces an angular positioning shift between
the motor and the rotor. This is because the hose cannot transfer shear forces when not
twisted. Twisting the hose would reduce its length. However, since the pressure from the
expansion fit is greater than the pull from the twisting, the hose rotates, transferring the
rotation from the motor to the rotor. This induces the angular positioning shift and must
be considered when data processing.
Furthermore, twisting the hose results in the hose
weakening over time due to constant shear. Hence, the hose must be frequently replaced so
that it does not break during experimental work.
3.2
Sensor and electronic equipment
The test rig is equipped with sensors measuring the disc displacement, angular position and
velocity of the rotor system, and flow and pressure of the air flowing through the gas bearing.
The angular velocity of the rotor system is controlled using an electric motor, and the airflow
through the gas bearing is controlled using piezo actuators.
3.2.1
Displacement Sensors
The displacement of the disc is measured using two proximity sensors placed perpendicular
to the outer surface of the disc. One of the sensors measures vertical displacement, a Vibro-
meter TQ402, and the second measures horizontal displacement, a Vibro-meter TQ442. These
are connected to signal conditioners, a Vibro-meter IQS450, converting the signals from the
proximity sensors to a DC voltage between 0 −10V.
3.2.2
Electric Motor
The motor used for rotating the rotor bearing system is a RTM80x73-2.2 electric motor
produced by Rattm Motor. The motor has a rated speed of between 0 −24000RPM and
is controlled by a FC-051-P1K5S2-E20H3BXCXXXSXXX Danfoss VLT Micro Drive, using
dSPACE to control the speed, by a voltage signal and a control box for start and stop of the
motor.
==PAGE:36==
26
3.2. Sensor and electronic equipment
3.2.3
Piezo Actuators
For controlling the airflow through the four nozzles of
the gas bearing, four piezo actuators, PICMA P-841.3,
are used. Each can be individually controlled through
voltage signals from dSPACE. They have a maximum
travelling distance of 45µm
3.2.4
Incremental Encoder
An incremental encoder is used to measure the angu-
lar position of the system. The encoder installed at
the beginning of the thesis was an Avago AEDB-9140
mounted between the ball bearing and the flexible cou-
pling to the motor, see Figure 3.7. The incremental
encoder has three output channels: channel I output
one pulse per rotation, and channels A and B output
500 pulses per rotation. Channel A and B each out-
put 250 pulses per rotation alternating between each
other. Channel I is used for calculating the rotational
speed and resetting channels A and B. Channels A
and B are used to calculate the angle of the rotor,
which is used for the run-out compensation described
in Section 4.3.6.
Figure 3.7: The Avago AEDB-9140
encoder, with a broken channel I ca-
ble.
3.2.5
Implementing a New Incremental Encoder
The incremental encoder described in Section 3.2.4 was replaced due to inadequate durability
of the soldering and connections between the sensor and dSPACE control module. The sol-
dering on the backside of the sensor was cracked such that the connection was still achievable
but unstable. The incremental encoder was replaced with the SCH50-720-N-08-34-65-02-S-
00-S3 from SCANCON Industrial Encoders. Sourced by Simon Friborg Mortensen for his
master thesis [5]. This is a hollow shaft encoder designed to have a shaft inserted through it.
Due to space restrictions between the motor and the shaft of the encoder and to avoid adding
extra mass and stiffness to the system, the bracket for the encoder would add stiffness. The
encoder was mounted on the backside of the motor. This can be seen in Figure 3.8
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.8: The new encoder mounted on a 3D-printed PLA bracket, bolted to the mounting
plate of the electric motor.
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 3. Test Rig Setup
27
The encoder was mounted on a connecting shaft screwed into the motor’s shaft through the
cooling fan’s mounting point and its protecting casing.
The connecting shaft hence acts
as a cooling fan fastener without compromising the motor’s airflow. The drawing for the
connecting shaft can be seen in Appendix A.1. For attaching the encoder to the test rig, a
bracket was designed to be attached to the mounting plate for the electric motor, a machine
drawing can be seen in Appendix A.2. A spring coupling was used to connect the encoder and
the bracket so that a minor eccentricity between the shaft and encoder could be permitted.
This and the data sheet for the encoder can be seen in Appendix A.3.
For connecting the encoder to the dSPACE control module, the wires from the encoder were
soldered to a D-sub 15-pin female connector and a D-sub 37-pin female connector.
The
connections followed the numbering scheme from [14] or seen in Figure 3.9. A table showing
the connections can be seen in Table 3.1. Since the new encoder has an angular resolution of
720 pulses per rotation and the old encoder had 500 pulses per rotation, the gain in dSPACE
for converting pulses to degrees was changed from 360/500 to 360/720, i.e. (360 degrees over
the pulses per rotation).

3.3
dSPACE
The dSPACE ControlDesk is a software that enables the control of and data acquisition
from electrical sensors, motors, etc., using the dSPACE control module through low voltage
signals. The dSPACE ControlDesk uses a SIMULINK model to create the control and data
acquisition code. Ask Finderup and Vanja Elizabeth Wylie created the SIMULINK model
used for this thesis as a part of their master thesis in the spring of 2022 [15].
3.4
Alignment and Calibration of the Test Rig
3.4.1
Calibration of the Piezo Actuators
To obtain accurate results, the piezo actuator’s initial distance from the actuator head to the
orifice wall had to be calibrated. This is done to ensure when the actuators are supplied with
maximum voltage, the airflow through the orifice is completely closed. The calibration is done
==PAGE:38==
28
3.4. Alignment and Calibration of the Test Rig
by adjusting the distance of the piezo actuator body to the side of the bearing housing. The
calibrations are done such that when 10V is supplied to a piezo actuator control amplifier,
the piezo actuator is fully extended. The body of the piezo is moved inwards until there is no
airflow through the orifice. A calibration tool is used to achieve the accuracy needed, as seen
in Figure 3.10a. The tool is mounted to each piezo, as seen in Figure 3.10b. The distance
is adjusted, and when the airflow is closed, the piezo is supplied with different voltages, for
example, 9V, to ensure that the piezo is calibrated correctly since air should be able to pass
through when not prescribing 10V. The tool is removed, and the piezo actuators are then
fastened into place.
(a) Calibration tool, a micrometer mounted
to an aluminium bracket.
(b) The Calibration tool mounted on the test
rig.
Figure 3.10
3.4.2
Alignment of the Setup
Alignment of the bearings and the shaft has to be done due to the aluminium plate supporting
the bearings and shaft. The plate is twisting, which misaligns the gas bearing and the ball
bearing such that the shaft is in contact with the side of the gas bearing. To realign the
bearings, the six bolts fastening the aluminium plate to the test stand, as seen in Figure 3.11,
had to be tightened and loosened for straightening the plate. To quantify the effect of the
tightening and loosening of the bolts, a dial gauge was used. The dial gauge was placed, as
seen in Figure 3.12, touching the corner side of the bearing house. This was done to measure
the twisting of the bearing house with the increase and decrease in the tightening torque of
the bolts. During the alignment the motor was disconnected from the shaft and all valves
were opened with a pressure of 3bar. The bolts were tightened and loosened by an iterative
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 3. Test Rig Setup
29
trial and error scheme until the shaft spun freely. It was found that increasing the torque in
bolt I to III resulted in rotating the bearing house clockwise and increasing the torque in bolt
IV to VI counter-clockwise. When the shaft spun freely at 3bar with all valves open, valves
2 and 4 were closed, and the tightening-loosening scheme was done again until the axis spun
freely. The same procedure was used when increasing the pressure to 7bar. If adjustments
were made, all valves and pressure configurations had to be tested again since an adjustment
at one pressure affected the alignment at the other pressures.
Figure 3.11: The test rig from above, with bolt numbering
Figure 3.12: The dial gauge mounted on a magnetic stand attached to the steel testing stand.
==PAGE:40==
30
3.4. Alignment and Calibration of the Test Rig
==PAGE:41==
4
Experimental Procedure
This chapter describes the selection of parameters for the experimental work, the planning
of the experiments, the experimental procedure for OMA and EMA experiments, and the
challenges and limitations under the experimental work.
4.1
Selection of Test Parameters
The parameters chosen to be used in the experimental work were chosen based on a combi-
nation of what had been done in previous projects, [3], [15], and [5], and what was deemed
interesting to study. The parameters chosen are mostly the same regardless of the choice of
analysis, OMA or EMA. The parameters that are specifically for one of the analysis types
will be marked by the analysis type in parentheses.
Using the 2k factorial design theory from Section 2.3.2, the parameters chosen are:
Angular Velocity: The rotor system angular velocity was chosen as one of the three values:
1000, 3000, and 5000RPM. 5000RPM was chosen to be the upper limit since it is known
from the analysis shown in [3, fig. 4.6] that above 5000RPM, the rotor system is entering the
beginning of the critical speed range.
Furthermore, a small FEM study is conducted and can be seen in Appendix B.1. This is not
in the main report due to being widely inaccurate when comparing results obtained by [5].
The inaccuracy was due to the modelling of the gas bearing being based on the studies done
by [3], where the nominal bearing clearing was 25µm compared to 35.1µm found in Section
5.1. Since the bearing clearance has increased, the results obtained by [3] are no longer viable
for practical purposes. The limitation for the upper angular velocity is still used since no
critical speed study was done due to time limitations.
1000RPM was chosen to ensure that the rotor bearing system is in the hybrid lubrication
regime. 3000RPM was chosen to have an extra point between the high and low rotational
speeds after conducting all tests at 1000 and 5000RPM. Choosing an extra rotational speed
is in contradiction with the 2k factorial design described in Section 2.3.2. This is dealt with
by creating a series of experiments where the rotational speed is kept constant at 3000RPM;
this can be seen in Section 4.2.3.
Pressure: For each test, the air in the gas bearing was pressurised to either 3 or 7bar. 3bar
was chosen since this is the lowest pressure where the rotor system can spin freely without
being in contact with the wall of the bearing. 7bar was chosen since this is the constant upper
limit of the supply pressure.
DC-Offset: This is the constant voltage that the piezo actuators were prescribed and defines
how open the valves are; at 10V, the valves are completely closed. This value was set to either
0 or 5V, as was done in [3].
Injector Configurations: Two injection configurations are tested. Case I, where injectors
1 and 3 are open and case II where all injectors are open, see Figure 3.5 for injector positions.
Excitation Amplitude (OMA): The excitation amplitude is the amplitude of the voltage
signals to the piezo actuators and was chosen to be 1.0 or 2.5V. This was done since the
OMA Applied to Rotor-Bearing Systems via Pneumatic Actuation - Low Damping and Air
31
==PAGE:42==
32
4.2. Experimental Plan
excitation should be small enough to not change the dynamics of the system, but should also
be large enough to excite the system. The choice of range was chosen based on an excitation
study done by [15], where the ideal excitation was found to be 1.5V. However, this study
was done at zero rotational speed, thereby not considering the system’s dynamics. Hence the
chosen values are a little lower and higher to see what influence they have on the estimation
of the modal parameters.
Number of Valves Excited (OMA): The number of valves used to excite the system was
chosen to be either valve 1 or valves 1, 2, and 4. Valve 1 was chosen to be used since this
will excite the vertical movement of the rotor system, thereby favouring the auto-correlation
for the vertical movement. The excitement from valve 1 will excite the forward mode of the
system, making this the dominant mode at these tests. Valve 1, 2, and 4 were chosen to see
if this had any improvement on the estimation of the cross-correlation and auto-correlation
for the horizontal and vertical movement, thereby improving the whole correlation function
and maybe improving the estimations of the modal parameters. Further excitation from the
side valves might also improve the estimation of the backward mode of the system.
Note that the number of valves excited cannot exceed the number of injectors open, i.e. in
injector case I, the excitation valve can only be valve 1.
Excitation Direction (EMA): The excitation direction describes the direction of the
impact-hammer impact, vertical or horizontal.
Impacting the rotor system vertically ex-
cites the forward mode, favouring the estimation of the forward natural frequencies and
corresponding damping ratios. Exciting the rotor system horizontally excites the backward
modes, favouring the estimation of the backward natural frequencies and damping ratios.
For all configurations, the injectors are equally pressurised and the displacement of the
mounted disc is recorded in the horizontal and vertical direction.
4.2

An experimental plan must be created and completely randomised for conducting the exper-
iments of a 2k factorial design. The parameters chosen are, by convention, denoted as capital
Latin letters. Thus ”A” refers to the angular velocity, ”B” refers to the injection pressure,
etc. These model terms and the factor levels can be seen in Table 4.1:

4.2.3
Phase III
Phase III is a replication of all experiments in phases I and II at 3000 rpm replicated four
times, resulting in 32 EMA and 96 OMA experiments. This was done as stated in Section
4.1 to have an extra data point between the angular velocity’s high and low levels. The
extra data point confirms or denies the assumption in 2.3.2 that the response between the
low and high level for the angular velocity is linear. The experiments in this phase are split
into three parts: 1. replicating EMA, 2. replicating the experiments with excitation from
valves 1, 2, and 4. 3. replicating the experiments from phase I. The design matrices and
the corresponding completely randomised experiment tables can be seen in Appendix B.4.1,
B.4.2, and B.4.3.
The combined number of tests for the three phases is 288 OMA tests and 96 EMA tests.
4.3
OMA Experimental Procedure
This section describes the consideration, experimental procedure, and the following analysis
when performing operational modal analysis experiments.
==PAGE:46==
36
4.3. OMA Experimental Procedure
4.3.1
Sampling Rate
The sampling rate defines the frequency band’s upper limit and the maximum frequency where
no more information about the structure can be determined. The upper limit frequency band
is given by the Nyquist frequency [6, eq. (7.10)]:
fv = fs/2,
where:
fs = 1/∆t
(4.1)
where fs and ∆t are the sampling frequency and sampling time step, respectively.
It is
recommended that the sampling frequency must be larger than 1.2 times fv, and hence the
recommended sampling frequency becomes:
fs > 2.4fmax
(4.2)
where fmax is the highest frequency of interest. From the previous studies, [15] and [5], fmax is
chosen to be 250Hz. This is chosen as a conservative maximum frequency to be able to obtain
modal information for the first mode of the system. This results in a minimum sampling rate
of:
fs > 600Hz
(4.3)
4.3.2
Length of Time Series
To ensure good estimation from the OMA techniques, the time series length must be long
enough to capture all vital information.
There are two main methods of estimating the
minimum time series length. The first is the ANSI S2.47, which states that for a bias error of
4% and the variance error of 10%, then the times series must have the length [6, eq. (7.3)]:
T = 200
ζf
(4.4)
where f is the fundamental frequency in Hz and ζ it corresponding modal damping factor.
Another method of estimating the minimum time series length is to use the maximum cor-
relation time in the response. Assuming that the longest correlation time is defined by the
lowest natural frequency of the system, yields [6, eq. (7.16)]:
T >
10
ζfmin
(4.5)
where fmin is the lowest natural frequency in Hz and ζ it corresponding modal damping ratio.
Using the minimum and maximum natural frequencies for the forward and backward mode
shapes obtained by [5, tab. 11, 12], and equation (4.4) and (4.5) varying the damping ratio
from 0.001 < to 20%, Figure 4.1 is obtained. The minimum and maximum natural frequency
for the forward mode used are 92Hz and 146.7Hz and the minimum and maximum natural
frequency for the backward mode used are 80.7Hz and 126Hz.
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 4. Experimental Procedure
37
0
20
40
60
80
100
120
140
160
180
200
Time Series Length [s]
0.03
0.05
0.1
0.2
0.5
1
2
5

Damping Ratio [%]
Length of Time Series

Min. Nat. Freq. Forward Mode

Figure 4.1: Length of time series as a function of the damping ratio, full lines represent the
time length calculations using (4.4) and dashed lines using (4.5)
From the figure, it was determined that the time series should be 120s to be able to capture
information of modes with low damping, according to equation (4.5) and moderate damping,
according to equation (4.4).
4.3.3
Number of Sensors
The minimum number of sensors needed is a rank problem. The measurement system should
be able to clearly identify all important sources of noise and physical response so that the
measurement system does not limit the rank of the problem. Therefore the number of mea-
surement channels, the number of sensors, should be larger than the number of noise sources
and closely spaced modes. Furthermore, these measurement channels should be spread over
the structure to minimise the chance of contributing the same data. A sensor should never be
placed in a node of any mode of interest since it would not be able to obtain any information
for that mode. According to [6], one should never assume that a rank of a problem is under
four, and a good rule of thumb is always to use at least five or six sensors.
As stated in Section 3.2.1 the number of sensors used is two. Due to time limitations, it
was impossible to source new sensors, design new brackets for them, and implement them in
dSPACE. Hence using two sensors had to be suﬀicient. A method for countering the small
number of sensors is introducing noise modes to enhance the results; this is described further
in Section 4.3.6.
4.3.4
Excitation
The excitation when using OMA was applied through the gas bearing nozzles and consisted of
pressurised air in a white noise pattern. The pattern was created by opening and closing the
airflow through the nozzle using a white noise voltage signal. To ensure that both the white
noise signal and the output of the piezo are white noise, they were investigated. Three of
the chosen parameters from Section 4.1 were determined to possibly affect the piezo position
when writing a white noise signal to the piezo. These are DC-offset, pressure, and excitation
amplitude at zero rotational speed. These were varied as chosen in 4.1, DC-offset = [0,5] V,
Pressure = [3,7] bar and Excitation Amplitude = [1,2.5] V. This resulted in eight tests, and
two of the most interesting are shown down below; the rest can be found in Appendix B.5,
==PAGE:48==
38
4.3. OMA Experimental Procedure
due to being very similar to the ones shown. Furthermore, Figure B.1 and B.5 are completely
Gaussian distributed in all piezo positions and displacements; this is exactly what is wanted
but is deemed not to be especially interesting to discuss. The first test of interest is Piezo
test 002:
Figure 4.2: DC-Offset = 0 V, Pressure 3 bar, and Excitation Amplitude = 1 V
As can be seen from Figure 4.2, the signal is Gaussian distributed. However, when inputted
into the piezo, the position of the piezo becomes Poison distributed. This does not seem
to affect the horizontal or vertical displacement of the rotor since the displacement is still
Gaussian distributed. The general trend, see Figure B.6 in Appendix B.5, is that when the
DC-offset is 0V and the excitation amplitude is 2.5V the distribution of the position of the
valve becomes Poison distributed. However, the displacement is still Gaussian.
The second test of interest is Piezo test 003 seen in Figure 4.3:
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 4. Experimental Procedure
39
Figure 4.3: DC-Offset = 5 V, Pressure 3 bar, and Excitation Amplitude = 2.5 V
From Figure 4.3 it can be seen that even though the input signal to piezo 1 is Gaussian
distributed, the position of the piezo is permanent 10µ. This contradicts the observations
done when calibrating the piezos, as done in Section 3.4.1, where when the piezo is given
a white noise signal, the piezo vibrates, and it can be observed that the piezo head moves.
Furthermore, under calibrating where the piezo was set up to control the airflow, it could be
observed that the airflow was changed when actuating the piezo with a white noise signal. This
permanent position might result from the piezo moving with a higher frequency than 2500Hz,
making it impossible for dSPACE to capture. Despite seeming stuck in one position, it excites
Gaussian distributed movements in the vertical direction with the same magnitude as Piezo
test 002. This is the general trend for all the other experiments where DC-Offset = 5V, see
Figure B.4, B.7, and B.8 in Appendix B.5.
With all the results from the eight tests, it can be concluded that giving a white signal to the
piezos induces white noise displacement to the rotor-bearing system.
4.3.5
Experiemtnal Procedure
For all experiments, the rotor was spun up to the desired angular velocity. To prolong the life
of the flexible coupling, the angular velocity of the rotor system has to be steadily increased
==PAGE:50==
40
4.3. OMA Experimental Procedure
to decrease the stress on the hose. When the rotor has the desired angular velocity, and the
system has stabilised, a run-out measurement was performed without the piezos activated.
This was done to be able to make run-out compensation to remove the unbalance of the
system from the data.
After the run-out measurement, the piezos were activated at the
desired amplitude, and the experiment was performed.
4.3.6
Data Processing
The subsequent data processing is done to estimate the modal parameters when the data is
obtained.
Run-Out Compensation
For removing the effect of the unbalance of the rotor system from the recorded data, a run-out
compensation is conducted for each test.
The compensations are conducted by spinning the rotor at the desired rotational speed for the
test, without any excitation added to the system. The displacement and angular position are
measured over 10 seconds of rotation using the displacement sensors and the encoder. For each
recorded angle, the mean of all the displacements at that angle is used as the compensation.
Due to the encoder being mounted to the motor and the flexible coupling between the motor
and the rotor, the run-out and the actual tests tend to be phase shifted. This is dealt with
by calculating the mean of the test displacement, and phase shifts the compensation data to
fit the mean of the displacement. The angle the data is shifted is found by phase shifting
the compensation data between -40 and 40 degrees and calculating the root mean square
error, RMSE, between the compensation data and the mean displacement data. The angle
of the lowest RMSE is the phase shift applied to the data. An example of this can be seen in
Figure 4.4:
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 4. Experimental Procedure

lations.
Number of Time-lags
The correlation function is obtained as described in Section 2.1.4, and the number of time-
lags is determined as described in Section 2.1.1. When the data in the correlation function
has decayed, all the information of the system is in the non-decayed correlation function and
using the decayed part will result in noise of the estimate. Hence the number of time lags
has to be determined to ensure that only the non-decayed part of the correlation function
is captured. This is done manually for each experiment. An example of this can be seen in
Figure 4.7:
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 4. Experimental Procedure

number of time-lags for all experiments can be seen in Appendix B.7 Table B.19 and the
correlation function matrix can be seen plotted for all tests in AppendixB.8.
OMA Algorithm Parameter Variation
When applying the OMA techniques there are besides the number of time-lags, two main
parameters that can be varied: noise modes (number of modes estimated) and the number of
s block rows.
Noise Modes
A general rule of thumb is not to estimate more modes than measuring
channels of the system. Since only two sensors are used, there can only be estimated two
modes. This cannot be fulfilled since, in many cases, only using two modes results in sig-
nificant deviations between the estimated correlation function and the system’s correlation
function. Hence to compensate for this, noise modes are added to the system, i.e. oversizing
the system.
The number of noise modes, also known as the number of modes detected, is used to enhance
the OMA techniques estimation of the modal parameters.
The noise modes enhance the
estimation by forcing the technique to determine modes in between the physical modes refining
the physical modes by removing noise parts from the physical mode estimates.
The noise modes do typically have a low participation factor. The participation factor de-
scribes the contribution of the different modes to the estimated correlation function. Since
noise modes do not have any physical meaning, they usually have a low participation factor
in contrast to physical modes with a high physical meaning and hence a high participation
factor.
s block rows
The number of s block rows determines how much data is used to estimate
the correlation function. This can be seen from the block Hankel matrices, equation (2.23),
(2.29), and (2.39). This is varied to determine how much data creates the best estimation. A
drawback of using more data is the increase in calculation time.
Determining the Number of Noise Modes and s block rows
The number of s block
rows and noise modes, n, is determined based on how well the estimation fits the correlation
==PAGE:54==
44
4.3. OMA Experimental Procedure
function. The number of s block rows is increased from 2 to 25. The number of s block
rows starts at two since having fewer rows than measuring channels is impossible. Since it
is impossible to detect more modes than s block rows, the number of modes detected is n =
[1, 2, ..., s]. Due to computational time, the maximum number of noise modes was restricted
to 25. With an increase in s block rows the block Hankel also increases. Hence it becomes
increasingly more time-consuming to calculate the modal parameters. Some improvements to
the calculations were made, as can be seen in Section 4.3.7. For each number of s block rows
and noise modes, the estimation of the correlation function is calculated. This estimation
is compared to the inputted correlation function, and the root mean square error, RMSE,
of the difference between the two is calculated. When all combination of s and n has been
computed, the one with the smallest RMSE is chosen to represent the best estimation of
modal parameters. An example of this can be seen in Figure 4.8 for OMA Test 001, ERA
calculation.
Figure 4.8: The number of s block rows and the number of modes detected, with their
corresponding RMSE, OMA Test 001, ERA calculation. The green ring indicates the smallest
root mean square error between the estimated and inputted correlation functions.
The number of s block rows and noise nodes n for all experiments can be seen in Appendix
B.9 Table B.20.
Results
The result of the OMA techniques are matrices of estimations of the natural frequencies with
their corresponding damping ratios and participation factors, a matrix of mode shapes and
the estimation of the correlation function. An example, the estimation of the auto-correlation
for the vertical displacement for OMA test 001, can be seen in Figure 4.9:

4.3.7
Improvements to the OMA Calculations
New Block Hankel Calculation for SSI
Due to slow computational time calculating the block Hankel matrix for the SSI technique,
seen in equation (2.39), for large time series and a large number of rows and columns, the
code was improved. The original construction of the matrix was done using two for-loops,
with one for the columns and one for the rows, constructing the matrix one element at a time.
This is an acceptable method when the data series are small. This is not the case for large
data series, where the block Hankel matrix consists of thousands or millions of elements. The
new construction of the matrix is done using matrix calculations. First, a matrix consisting
of the indexing in of y as seen in equation (2.39).
After that, all the elements of y are
inputted into the block Hankel matrix in one operation. This improved the computational
time significantly, as can be seen in Figure 4.10:
==PAGE:56==
46
4.4. EMA Experimental Procedure

Figure 4.10: Ratio between the old and new block Hankel construction time
The figure is constructed by averaging 20 2 × 30000 time series, increasing the number of s
block rows until 150. From the figure, it can be seen that the improvement in calculation
time is logarithmic to the number of the s block rows, and is converging toward 23.
The old and new MATLAB code can be found in Appendix B.10.

This section describes the experimental procedure and the following analysis when performing
experimental modal analysis experiments.
4.4.1
Excitation
The excitation was done by impacting the brass bushing behind the mounted disc, which can
be seen on Figure 4.11a, using an impact hammer. The impact hammer is fitted with a plastic
tip, and a force transducer is mounted to the hammerhead, used to measure the impact. The
hammer can be seen in Figure 4.11b.

The bushing is hit 30 times per test, either vertically or horizontally. The impact was timed
using a metronome set to 0.5Hz to make the time between the impacts as uniform as possible.
The desired impact force was between 100 and 200 N, as this would not displace the rotor
excessively, hitting the bearing wall and damaging the bearing. Several other projects have
done this, [15] and [5], and have resulted in reasonable EMA estimates. Hence this should
be suitable for this thesis as well. To ensure that the time between each hit was suﬀicient for
the system to return to its steady state. The impacts of EMA Test 001 were investigated.
Every impact was studied for its vertical and horizontal displacement; this can be seen in

Raw Data, EMA Test - 001
Figure 4.13: Raw force and displacement data from vertical impacts, EMA Test 001
The raw data do not convey any meaningful information by themselves and are often indis-
tinguishable from each other. Hence the raw data is only shown here.
The raw data is run-out compensated, using the same technique described in Section 4.3.6
and a low pass Butterworth filter is applied to remove high-frequency electronic noise. The
data is segmented such that the data around each impact is its own segment. A Hanning
window is applied to scale the data according to a cosine function enhancing data of high
amplitude and decreasing the noise of the data, data of low amplitude. After the auto- and
==PAGE:59==
Chapter 4. Experimental Procedure
49
cross-correlation are calculated for each segment. These are Fourier transformed into the
auto-spectral density and cross-spectral density, respectively. The auto-spectral density and
cross-spectral densities are hereafter averaged over all segments and used to calculate the
transfer functions H1(ω) and H2(ω), following equation (2.55). This method is known as the
Welch Method.
As described in Section 2.2 the next step is to estimate the natural frequencies and damping
ratios. This is done by using the single-degree-of-freedom, SDOF, method. In this method,
each peak of the frequency response function is treated as a response of a single degree of
freedom system. By treating the response this way, it can be assumed that in the vicinity of
a resonance peak, the frequency response function is dominated by that single mode. This is
done by first defining the frequency span to look in, approx 40Hz to 170Hz, since it is known
that the first damped natural frequency of the system is within this range, [3], [15], and [5].
Then determining the five highest peaks of H1(ω) and H2(ω), The frequencies where these
peaks occur are the damped natural frequencies of the system and are sorted based on the
height of the peaks. The damping ratio, ζ, for each associated peak is obtained by assuming
that the system’s damping is small enough, such that the peak of |H(ω)| at resonance is well
defined. Hence the damping ratio is related to the frequencies corresponding to the two points
of the frequency response function [7, eq. (7.13)]:
|H(ωa)| = |H(ωb)| = |H(ωd)|
√
2
(4.6)
This results in the damping ratio being defined as [7, eq. (7.14)]:
ωb −ωa = 2ζωd
⇒
ζ = ωb −ωa
2ωd
(4.7)
where ωd is the damped natural frequency and ωa and ωb satisfies equation (4.6). This is
called the 3-dB down point condition, since |H(ωd)|
√
2
corresponds to 3dB below the resonance
peak. For a visual representation see Figure 4.14:

4.5
Challenges and Limitations with the Test Rig
Various challenges and limitations were encountered throughout the project using and work-
ing with the test rig.
These challenges were time-consuming and limited the number of
experiments and the time planned for the data processing. These challenges will be discussed
below.
4.5.1
Incremental Encoder
As mentioned in Section 3.2.5 the original incremental encoder was replaced due to inadequate
durability of the soldering. The soldering on the original encoder was detaching itself from
the stripboard, and the wires connecting the encoder with the dSPACE module were worn out
and kept breaking from the soldering. This was fixed multiple times over a two-week period,
interrupting the experimental phase I. Hence reattaching and re-soldering the wiring were
deemed too time-consuming and the encoder too fragile for this project. Hence the decision
was taken to replace the old encoder with the new and better encoder, as described in Section
3.2.5. Phase I had to be re-conducted such that all experiments had the same angular quality.
4.5.2
Wear on the Test Rig
As a part of Christian Tobias Simony Nielsen’s master thesis on the test rig, the rotor was
separated from the rest of the test rig; to measure the rotor’s dimensions. Doing this revealed
significant wear marks on the bearing wall and rotor sleeve, as seen in Figure 4.16.
Consequently, the bearing and rotor were handed over to the workshop for polishing after the
rotor measurements were obtained. This routine procedure was estimated to take one to two
days, but due to sickness was prolonged to almost two weeks. Furthermore, by polishing, the
==PAGE:61==
Chapter 4. Experimental Procedure
51
bearing and sleeve material is removed from both. This results in the clearance of the bearing
increasing rendering the EMA results obtained by Simon Friborg Mortensen not usable for
comparing the OMA results obtained in phase I. This meant that a series of EMA experiments
had to be conducted to verify the results obtained using OMA, further reducing the number
of OMA experiments and data processing time.
(a) Wear marks on the bearing bushing.
(b) Wear marks on the rotor sleeve.
Figure 4.16
4.5.3
Alignment of the Test Rig
Due to the dismantling of the rotor bearing system, the rotor and the bearing had to be
re-aligned. This was an iterative and time-consuming task where the bolts were tightened
and loosened based on how well the rotor rotated in the bearing. After multiple attempts
over multiple days, the technique described in Section 3.4.2 was developed for quantifying the
effect of loosening and tightening the bolts, reducing the amount of guessing and hence the
time used on alignment.
4.5.4
Number of Sensors
As stated in Section 4.3.3 the number of sensors for the test rig is only two, which is far
below the minimum number of six sensors. This increases data processing time since even a
small amount of noise in one of the displacement data sets increases the chances of estimating
inaccurately significantly. The noise increases the data processing time since almost all noise
must be removed. Furthermore, only using two sensors makes it impossible to determine the
mode shapes of the rotor-bearing system, and therefore, these are not studied in this thesis.
4.5.5
Excitation of the System using the Gas Bearing
As stated in Section 2.1.1, the system’s excitation has to be Gaussian distributed, and the
excitation has to be distributed over the whole structure. This is not the case since using the
gas bearing to excite the structure excites the structure at one point. This has the implication
that if this point is a node for a specific mode of interest, this mode can not be excited.
==PAGE:62==
52
4.5. Challenges and Limitations with the Test Rig
4.5.6
Time used on Experimental Work

time used on the individual test experiment groups can be seen in Table 4.9.
