The displacement must be projected from the disc to the bearing to calculate the bearing
clearance. This is done by assuming that the shaft pivots around the ball bearing. Using
the displacement of the disc and the distance from the ball bearing to the disc (438mm), the
angle can be calculated. Using the angle and the distance from the ball bearing to the gas
bearing (345mm), the clearance of the bearing can be determined. This results in the polar
clearance plots, as can be seen in Figure 5.2:
OMA Applied to Rotor-Bearing Systems via Pneumatic Actuation - Low Damping and Air
53

270, and 0 degrees respectively.

for test 005, which has the rough shape of a parallelogram. Despite this, the average of the
measurement is still at the same magnitude as the other test. The average of the tests and
the total average with a 95% confidence interval can be seen in Table 5.1.

As shown in Figure 5.3, using the different OMA techniques results in approximately the same
natural frequencies and mainly the same damping ratios, with some significant deviation for
the Ibrahim Time Domain technique.
Using ITD results in large deviations for the natural frequency at 3bar, with valves 1 and
3 open, 5000RPM and an excitation amplitude of 1V.
Here the technique estimates the
unbalance of the systems, 5000RPM = 83.3Hz, for both 0 and 5V DC-offset. The unbalance
of the system should have been filtered from the response by the run-out compensation. The
compensated data and the correlation function for the two tests are studied to investigate
why the unbalance is estimated. Using Table 4.4 the tests are found to be 003 and 024 and

This could result from the excitation amplitude not being suﬀicient to excite the rotor-bearing
system. Hence response is dominated by the remnants of the unbalance response.

found to be tests 026, 027, and 030 and are investigated to determine the reason for this
estimation.
It was found to be the number of time lags for tests 026 and 027.

For OMA tests 026 and 027, the wrong estimations could be corrected easily by removing
the noise tail of the correlation function. Unfortunately, this was discovered too late in the
process to be able to rectify. The ITD estimation for test 030 does not correctly estimate
the auto-correlation since no stable modes were found with the correct natural frequency and
damping. Hence resulting in an approximately correct natural frequency but a very high
damping ratio.
When analysing the results from unreplicated experiments of a 2k factorial design, there
is no internal estimation of error. Hence using a normal probability plot of the estimated
effects provides a simple method for overcoming no internal error estimation [6, p. 255]. The
negligible effects are Gaussian distributed, with zero mean and variance, and will tend to
follow a straight line. Significant effects and interactions will have a nonzero mean and will
lie outside of the straight line. Furthermore, if non-significant effects do not follow a straight
line, this usually indicates that an outlier or multiple outliers are present in the data.
The effects are calculated as presented in Section 2.3 using the contrast constant table seen
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 5. Results and Discussion
59

to high level, results in the effect seen. OMA Test 001 - 032. A table showing the exact value

It is clear from the figure that the main effect of all the techniques is the injection pressure;
increasing the pressure increases the natural frequency. For all the techniques, increasing the
DC-offset reduces the natural frequency and, except for ITD, increases the damping ratio.
Changing the rotational speed increases the damping ratio significantly for the ITD and, to
a lesser degree, for the other techniques. Furthermore, the magnitude of the higher-order
interactions for the ITD technique can be seen to be significant. This contradicts the rest of
the techniques, where the magnitudes of higher-order effects are negligible.
The effects are then used to create the normal probability plots for both natural frequencies
and damping ratios for all OMA techniques. These can be seen in Figure 5.11

Normal Probability Plot
Figure 5.11: Normal probability plot with significant elements marked with their respective
model term, OMA tests 001 - 032
From Figure 5.11 it is observed that the non-significant effects of the ITD do not follow
a straight line for the frequency and damping plot. In the frequency plots, the effects are
grouped into three main groups, and in the damping plot, there are two main groups. These
groupings are a strong indication that outliers are present in the estimations. In the rest of
the techniques, the non-significant effects follow a straight line for both the damping ratios
and frequencies. The only main difference between the estimations of the natural frequencies
and damping ratios are tests 003, 024, 026, 027, and 030. Hence these must be the outliers.
Furthermore, it can be seen from Figure 5.11 that the natural frequency estimation of the
system is mainly influenced by the injection pressure, B, the excitation amplitude, E, and the
DC-offset, C. The damping estimation is influenced mainly by the injection pressure and the
DC-offset. It can be concluded from tests 001 to 032, that the natural frequency of the system
is increased and the damping is decreased when increasing the injection pressure. This agrees
with the theory that increasing the pressure increases the system’s stiffness. It is observed
that increasing the DC-offset decreased the natural frequency estimation and increased the
damping estimation. This is also in agreement with theory since increasing the DC-offset
decreases the airflow through the bearing, decreasing the stiffness and increasing damping.
At last, it can be concluded that increasing the excitation amplitude increased the natural
frequency estimation. Increasing the amplitude results in a larger valve opening, letting more
air flow through the bearing and increasing the stiffness of the gas bearing.
It should be noted that the number of significant effects is very high for ITD and very low
for the other techniques. The techniques do not agree on significant interactions or their
==PAGE:71==
Chapter 5. Results and Discussion
61
signs. Hence there is a significant risk that since there are no replications of the individual
experiments, the estimates obtained are imprecise. This and the outliers when using the ITD
technique indicate that replications of the experiments are required for verification. Hence
phase II has to be conducted.
5.3
Phase II
As described in Section 4.2.2, phase II is a three-part phase, consisting of:
Part 1 consists of three replications of phase I. The main objective of part 1 is to replicate
the experiments from phase I to obtain more estimates for determining how consistent the
estimates are for the different OMA techniques and to determine the influence of the chosen
test parameters.
Part 2 consists of four replications of phase I with valves 1, 2, and 4 exciting the system.
This is done to determine if the estimations changes when comparing with only using valve
1 for exciting the system.
Part 3 consists of four replications of the parameters from phase I using EMA. This is done
to determine how accurate the OMA techniques estimates are since EMA estimates generally
are more robust and obtain more accurate results.
5.3.1
Part 1
Using the same parameters as in phase I, Table 5.2 and conducting the experiments according
to Table B.5, results in 96 new data sets to estimate frequency and damping from. As with
phase I, only the frequencies and damping ratios of the modes with the highest participation
factors are of interest. These can be seen combined with the results obtained in phase I in
Figure 5.12. The figure is sorted based on valve and pressure configurations. The estimates
sorted based on the experimental number can be seen in Appendix C.2.1 Figure C.2 and C.3.

From the Figure 5.12, it can be observed that most OMA techniques result in approximately
the same natural frequencies and damping ratios. The ITD technique continually generates
large frequency and damping deviations from the other techniques at 5000RPM, when the
excitation amplitude is 1V. The same tendency can be seen in the other frequency domain
techniques at 5000RPM and 3bar injection pressure. Here the techniques estimate the unbal-
ance frequency of the system with negligible damping.
As in phase I, the correlation functions are investigated; see Appendix B.8, Figure B.43,
B.48, B.56, B.65, B.69, B.70, B.107, and B.132. These correlation functions are found to
be of the same structure as tests 003 and 024 from phase I, with rapidly decaying vertical
auto-correlation functions. Hence enforcing the observation from phase I that the excita-
tion amplitude is insuﬀicient to excite the rotor-bearing system, such that frequency domain
techniques can consistently extract information about the system.

± 0.6
Table 5.3: Average, µ and 95% confidence interval, CI, for frequency and damping ratio esti-
mations, OMA test 001 - 128. The full-size table can be seen in Appendix subsubsection C.2.1
From the table, the most inconsistent of the techniques is the ITD. This technique estimates
consistently at low rotational speeds, i.e. small confidence interval. However, increasing the
rotational speed increases the confidence interval, i.e. the estimates become inconsistent. This
inconsistency is lowered when increasing the excitation amplitude. This is observed for both
the frequency and damping estimations. For the ERA and SSI techniques, the estimations
for the frequency and damping becomes inconsistent at high rotational speeds, low pressure,
and low excitation amplitude. At these configurations, the estimations are either in favour of
the unbalance of the system or rotor system, hence increasing the variance and, thereby, the
confidence interval.
The most consistent time-domain technique is the ERA technique, with an average 95%
confidence interval of ±4.1Hz and ±1.7% for the frequency and damping ratio estimations,
respectively. The most consistent of the techniques is the FDD, which does not estimate any
unbalance frequencies, and hence does not have any noticeable variance. The average 95%
confidence interval for the FDD frequency estimation is ±1.7Hz and ±0.6% for the damping
ratio estimations.
As in phase I, the factor effects are the first to be calculated for the statistical analysis. This is
done to examine the factor effects signs and magnitudes. This information is used to find the
most influential factors and interactions. The effects are calculated as described in Section
2.3 using the contrast constant table seen in Appendix C.1.1, Table C.1 and the resulting
effect estimations can be seen in Figure 5.13.

Effects, OMA Test 001-128
Figure 5.13: Frequency and damping effects estimations, increasing the parameters from low
to high level, results in the effect seen. OMA Test 001 - 128. A table showing the exact value
of the estimations can be seen in Appendix C.2.1, Table C.4
From the frequency effects from Figure 5.13 it is clear that factor B, injection pressure, is the
dominating effect, changing the pressure from 3bar to 7bar increases the natural frequency
by approximately 20Hz for all techniques. Factor B is followed by factors C and A, where
increasing the DC-offset or the pressure results in a decrease in the natural frequency. Fur-
thermore, all three and higher level interactions have negligible impact on the effects. Note
that ABD is identical to the BD interaction.
The damping ratio effects are more scattered, and there is no consistency in impact from
the large effects between the ITD and other techniques. For ITD, the main effects are A, D,
and E, and large effects are contributed to high-order interactions. This is in accordance to
[6, p. 255] unlikely since significant three-order interactions are rare and are found in 5% of
all experiments. Hence fourth and higher-order interactions are improbable. The damping
effects for ERA, SSI and FDD techniques are more conservative, and the dominating effects
are B and C, with small to negligible effects from three and higher-order interactions.
To statistically determine the main effects of the OMA estimations, the contribution and prob-
ability values, p-values, must be examined. These are found by calculating the ANOVA for
natural frequency and damping ratio estimations for all techniques. The individual ANOVAs
are found in Appendix C.2.1, Table C.5 to C.12. Note that the contributions are found by
dividing the sum of squares of the individual effects by the total sum of squares. The con-
tribution of the effects can be seen in Figure 5.14. All effects with a 1 percent or higher
contribution are in consideration for being a significant effect.
==PAGE:75==
Chapter 5. Results and Discussion
65
The p-value is used to measure the probability of confirming the null hypothesis, in this
case, the null hypothesis is that there is no difference when increasing or decreasing effects.
Hence effects with low p-values are significant. Since only significant values are desired, the
p-value is chosen to be p-value < 0.01, see Table 2.3. To determine the significant values the
1 −(p-value) plot, Figure 5.15 is created with a line at p-value = 0.01.

From Figure 5.14 and 5.15, it can be seen that lower-order interactions are the primary source
of effects with a high contribution or low p-values. The significant effects are chosen based
on whether they comply with a higher contribution factor than 1 percent and simultaneously
have a p-value under 0.01. This results in the following effect being classified as significant
effects:
Technique
Frequency Effects
Damping Effects
ITD
A, B, C, AB, BE, ABE

A, B, C, E, AB,
B, C
AE, BE, ABE

B, C, D
B, C, E, BE, ACD
Table 5.4: Significant effects, OMA Test 001-128
As expected from the analysis from Figure 5.13, higher-order interactions for ITD damping
estimations are significant. The rest of the significant effects for frequency and damping esti-
mations are mainly first and second-order interactions with limited third-order interactions.
The error contribution seen in Figure 5.14 is the noise not described by any effect. This is
significant for all time-domain techniques and is between 14 and 24 percent for the frequency
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 5. Results and Discussion
67
effects and between 21 and 34 percent for the damping effects. This is significant and confirms
what has already been found in Table 5.3, that the time domain estimations are non-consistent
in the estimations of the modal parameters.
The error is almost negligible for the FDD
technique, with 1.1 and 5.4 percent for the frequency and damping effects, respectively.
From the analysis, it can be concluded that the most consistent of the estimation techniques
for all combinations of parameters is the Frequency Domain Decomposition technique. This
is expected since a peak-picking process uses the peaks to band filter and remove non-picked
frequencies.
The ITD, ERA, and SSI technique results in consistent estimations at low angular velocity
but decreases in consistency at high rotational speeds. Furthermore, increasing the pressure
stabilises the estimations and results in adequate consistency. The significant effect for deter-
mining the estimates for the different techniques are presented in Table 5.4 and can be seen
to comprise of notably more effects for techniques with larger contributions of errors. The
high number of significant interactions for ITD, ERA, and SSI techniques is due to the high
errors of the effect estimations since this favours effects that might not be significant.
5.3.2
Part 2
This part consists of four replications of phase II part 1, with excitation from valves 1, 2, and
4. The parameters used are described in Table 4.5. These tests have test numbers from 129
to 192, and the parameters for the individual experiments can be seen in Appendix B.3.2,
Table B.7. This phase aims to determine if better estimates are found by exciting valves 1,
2, and 4. Since this requires that all valves are open, all previous tests with valves 1 and 3
open are disregarded in this analysis. The natural frequency and damping ratio estimations
from the test can be seen in Figure 5.16. The Estimation plotted by experimental number
can be seen in Appendix C.2.2, Figure C.4 and C.5.

From Figure 5.16 it can be seen that increasing the number of valves that excite the system
limits the estimations of the unbalance of the system to one ERA estimate. Increasing the
number of valves at 3bar increases the spread of the ITD frequency estimates and damping
estimates. Increasing the number of excitation valves at 7bar decreases the spread of the
estimates for frequency and damping ratio.

It is observed that the estimations of the natural frequencies are consistently the same for
both the forward and backward modes. The same can be concluded for the forward mode
damping ratio estimations. The estimation for the damping ratio for the backward mode
is more scattered. The estimations are consistent when all valves are open and when the
rotational speed is low. The backward mode is inconsistent in estimating the damping ratio
at low pressures and high rotational speeds. The inconsistency might be due to the peak of
the transfer functions not being well defined. This can be observed to be true for many of
the horizontal frequency response functions for horizontal excitation. These can be seen in
Appendix B.11, and two examples can be seen in Figure 5.19 and 5.20.

Figure 5.21: The Average of the natural frequency and damping ratio estimations with 95 %
confidence interval, EMA test 001-064, OMA test 001-192
As seen from the figure, the OMA estimations are clearly in the same range as the EMA
estimations. Furthermore, lowering the excitation amplitude increases the distance between
the OMA and EMA estimates. For further analysis, the residuals between the 95% confidence
interval of the OMA and EMA estimations are calculated. The residuals are determined as two
parts, a lower and an upper bound, LB and UB, respectively. The lower bound is calculated
by subtracting the upper 95% confidence interval for the OMA estimations from the lower
95% confidence interval for the EMA estimations. The upper bound is calculated the by
subtracting the lower 95% confidence interval for the OMA estimations from the upper 95%
==PAGE:84==

5.3. Phase II
confidence interval for the EMA estimations. Hence resulting in the two worst-case scenarios
for the 95% confidence interval. The residuals can be seen in Table 5.8:

Table 5.8: Lower and upper bound residuals, LB and UB, between EMA and OMA 95%
confidence interval, EMA test 001-064, OMA tests 001-192. The full table can be found in
Appendix C.2.3, Table C.22
From the table, it can be concluded that exciting the system with 1V at 5000RPM is inade-
quate for determining the frequency and damping for all bearing configurations when using
the ITD technique. The ERA and SSI estimations are also inadequate when the injection
pressure is 3bar and valves 1 and 3 are open. A general trend is when the injection pressure is
3bar, the residuals’ lower and upper bound are significantly larger than at 7bar. Furthermore,
it can be observed that increasing the excitation amplitude decreases average residuals for
both frequency and damping estimations considerably.
It can be concluded from the analysis of the accuracy of the OMA techniques that the ITD is
inadequate for precise estimations when the rotational speed is increased from 1000RPM to
5000RPM. Increasing the excitation amplitude from 1V to 2.5V decreases, on average, the
size of the 95% confidence interval and reduces the residuals between the OMA and EMA
estimations. Increasing the number of valves exciting the structure to three decreases the
residuals and the 95% confidence interval for the frequency estimations.
The most accurate of the OMA techniques was determined based on the overall accuracy
regardless of parameter configurations and the size of its 95% confidence interval. The most
accurate of the techniques used in this thesis is the Frequency Domain Decomposition with
the overall smallest confidence interval and smallest residuals. A strong contender to this
conclusion is the SSI estimations for the damping ratio. Here the confidence interval is larger
but is better centred around 0.

Chapter 5. Results and Discussion

5.4
Phase III
The objective of phase III is to check if the assumption of linearity holds when adjusting the
angular velocity to 3000RPM. This is done in three parts: an EMA repetition of phase II
part 3, an OMA repetition of phase II part 2, and an OMA repetition of phase I and phase
II part 1.
5.4.1
Part 1
The EMA experiments were conducted in accordance with Table B.12. The modal parameter
estimates combined with the estimates found in phase II part 3 results in Figure 5.22:

Figure 5.22: Natural frequencies and damping ratio estimations for EMA forward and back-

From the figure, it can be seen observed that as with the test conducted at 1000 and 5000RPM,
the new estimates are consistent in predicting the natural frequency for the forward and
backward modes and estimating the damping ratio for the forward mode.
The damping
ratios for the backward modes are inconsistent but follow the same scattering pattern as
with the 1000RPM and 5000RPM tests. It is observed as with the 1000RPM and 5000RPM
tests that the horizontal FRF is not well defined and hence results in inaccurate damping
estimations, see Appendix B.11, Figure B.361 to B.361. As in phase II, part 3, the backward
modes are due to time limitations, not analysed any further.
From Figure 5.22, it can be concluded that the linearity for the EMA test is fulfilled for the
forward mode shapes. It is more diﬀicult to conclude for the backwards modes since the
==PAGE:86==

estimates are scattered in three different patterns, and all seem linear. It should be noted
that since only four replications are conducted, the assumption of linearity can be diﬀicult to
confirm.

Figure 5.23: Estimations of the natural frequencies and damping ratios, for valve 1, 2, and 4

The figure shows that when conducting the tests at 3000RPM, the estimates are consistent
for the ERA, SSI, and FDD techniques. For the ITD estimates, when the injection pressure is
at 3bar are becoming inconsistent. The estimates for the natural frequency when applying an
excitation amplitude of 2.5V are beginning to scatter as observed at 5000RPM. The estimates
for the damping ratio when applying an excitation amplitude of 1.0V are increased to 50%
as can be observed at 5000RPM.
The assumption of linearity can, from Figure 5.23, be concluded to be valid for most of the
parameters. For the ITD technique, the variation of the estimates is too significant for any
decisive conclusion.

5.4.5
EMA and OMA Comparison
As in phase II part 3, Section 5.3.3, the accuracy of the OMA estimations is determined based
on their residuals to the EMA estimations. Again it is only the test with a DC-offset of 0V
that is used in this analysis since no EMA tests were conducted using 5V DC-offset.
To determine the accuracy of the OMA techniques, the residuals between the 95% confidence
interval of the OMA and EMA estimations are calculated. As in Section 5.3.3, the residuals
are determined as the lower and upper bound. The lower bound is calculated by subtracting
the upper 95% confidence interval for the OMA estimations from the lower 95% confidence
interval for the EMA estimations. The upper bound is calculated the by subtracting the lower
95% confidence interval for the OMA estimations from the upper 95% confidence interval for
the EMA estimations. Hence resulting in the two worst-case scenarios for the 95% confidence
interval. The residuals can be seen in Table 5.12:

5.5. Further investigations
==PAGE:93==
6
Conclusion, and Reflection
6.1
Conclusion
Throughout this project, four Operational Modal Analysis, OMA, techniques have been used
to estimate the modal parameters of the test rig at various angular velocities, injection pres-
sures, valve configurations, excitation amplitudes, and varying numbers of valves exciting the
system. The four OMA techniques were: Ibrahim Time Domain (ITD), Eigensystem Realisa-
tion Algorithm (ERA), Data-Driven Stochastic Subspace Identification (SSI), and Enhanced
Frequency Domain Decomposition (FDD). ITD, ERA, and SSI are time-domain techniques,
and FDD is a frequency-domain technique.
From phase I and phase II part 1, the most consistent of the time domain techniques, at
1000RPM and 5000RPM, is the SSI, and the most consistent of all the techniques is the
FDD. The technique with the worst consistency is the ITD which, at high angular velocities,
has a large confidence interval. The significant effects that influence the natural frequency
and damping ratio estimations were found to be:
Technique
Frequency Effects
Damping Effects
ITD
A, B, C, AB, BE,
A, B, D, E, AC, AD, AE, BC, CE,
ABE
DE, ABC, ACE, BCD, BCE, ADE
ERA
A, B, C, D, E, AB,
B, C, ABC
AE, BE, CE, ABE
SSI
A, B, C, E, AB,
B, C
AE, BE, ABE
FDD
B, C, D
B, C, E, BE, ACD
Table 6.1: Significant effects, OMA Test 001-128
Model
Factor
Term
A
Angular Velocity
B
Injection Pressure
C
DC-Offset
D
Valves Open
E
Excitation Amplitude
Table 6.2:
Modal Term
Explanation
The higher number of significant effects for the time domain OMA techniques was found to
be a result of the high contribution of the error in the effect estimations.
From phase II part 2, it was found that exciting the system with valves 1, 2, and 4 had a
statistically significant effect on the natural frequency estimations for the SSI technique and
did not significantly affect the damping estimations or for the other OMA techniques when
having a rotational speed of either 1000RPM or 5000RPM.
From phase II part III, it was concluded that the EMA technique is consistent in its esti-
mation of natural frequency and damping ratio for the forward mode and was consistent in
determining the natural frequency for the backward mode. Due to the peaks of the FRF not
being well defined, the damping estimations for the backward mode were concluded to be
inaccurate.
By comparing the OMA and EMA estimations, it was found that the most accurate of the
OMA techniques is the FDD which averages estimated the EMA estimations for the frequency
within −2.45Hz and 0.9Hz and the damping ratio within −1.32% and 0.07%.
The FDD
was followed by the SSI techniques, which average estimated the EMA frequency estimation
OMA Applied to Rotor-Bearing Systems via Pneumatic Actuation - Low Damping and Air
83
==PAGE:94==
84
6.2. Future Work
within −4.54Hz and 2.72Hz and the damping ratio estimation within −0.94% and 1.26%.
Further, it was concluded that increasing the excitation amplitude from 1V to 2.5V narrows
the confidence interval, increasing the accuracy of the estimations. At last, it was concluded
that the ITD is inadequate for estimating the natural frequency and damping ratio when the
excitation amplitude is 1V.
From phase III, the angular velocity was limited to 3000RPM, the assumption between linear
behaviour for the angular velocity was confirmed, and the 95% confidence interval was found
for all individual experiments.
It was found that exciting the system with valves 1, 2, and 4 significantly affected all OMA
techniques for both estimations of the natural frequency and damping ratio.
Through comparison between the EMA and OMA experiments, the most accurate of the
techniques was found to be the FDD with an average confidence interval for the damping
ratio estimation between 0.45% to 0.93% and for the natural frequency at −0.58Hz to 0.03Hz.
The FDD was followed by the ERA technique, the SSI and ITD. It was found that increasing
the excitation amplitude decreased the 95% confidence interval and thereby increased the
consistency and accuracy of the estimation techniques.
Hence it can be concluded that it is possible to track the change in natural frequencies and
damping ratio changes depending on the rotor’s angular velocity injection pressure of the gas
bearing and the valve configuration by using Operational Modal Analysis. On average, the
Enhanced Frequency Domain Decomposition technique is the most accurate technique in this
thesis.
6.2
Future Work
Upon completion of this project, several areas for improvement were recognised. These im-
provements are briefly described down below.
6.2.1
Improvement to the Test Rig
• Increase the number of displacement sensors; this could reduce the oversizing of the
block Hankel matrices used in OMA and used to estimate the mode shapes of the rotor
system.
• Separate the motor from the aluminium plate to reduce vibration interference between
the motor and the rotor-bearing system.
• Replace the aluminium plate of the test rig with a steel plate to reduce the plate’s
twisting and thereby reduce the time used on alignment.
• Automate the conduction of experiments in dSPACE using python. This would im-
prove.eﬀiciency of the experimental procedure, making it possible to conduct a larger
amount of experiments.
6.2.2
Improvements for OMA
• Investigate the backward modes of the system.
• To reduce the noise of the displacement signals, a Hanning window could be applied to
reduces the amount of noise from frequencies not of interest.
• Automating the identification of the correlation function’s noise tail will reduce the man-
ual data processing time and reduce the chance of determining the noise tail wrongly.
==PAGE:95==
Chapter 6. Conclusion, and Reflection
85
• Program the determination of the number of noise modes and s block rows in a more
eﬀicient programming language, such as FORTRAN, to reduce data processing time.
• Increase the number of factor levels and thereby experiments such that the system’s
behaviour can be modelled.
• Implementation of new OMA techniques, especially design for rotary dynamics. Since
the on used for this thesis are designed for non-rotating stationary constructions.
6.2.3
Improvement for EMA
• Increase the sampling rate around impacts to increase the resolution of the impact and
the resulting displacements.
• Re-calibrate the force transducer on the impact hammer since the peaks of the FRFs,
were not well defined.
• Re-conduct the experiments with horizontal impacts since these were not well defined.

A.3. SCH50-720-N-08-34-65-02-S-00-S3 Data sheet
==PAGE:107==
B
Experimental Procedure
B.1
The Flexible Rotor-Bearing System
This section aims to build a theoretical mechanical and mathematical model of the test rig.
This is done to predict the test rig’s natural frequencies, mode shapes, critical speeds and
stability before the experimental procedure. To do this before testing gives an approximation
of where to look for the system’s natural frequencies and, most importantly, for the healthiness
of the test rig, the first critical speed and the instability speed. The first critical speed and
the instability speed are essential since they indicate how fast the rotor can rotate without
risking damage to the test rig.
B.1.1
Mechanical Model
The theoretical model is made using the finite element method; hence some assumptions have
to be made:
• Shear is neglected; the rotor system is modelled as a Bernoulli-Euler beam.
• Homogeneous elements, all elements’ centre of mass are in the geometrical centre of the
cylindrical beam.
• Vibrations in the x-direction are negligible since they are much smaller than the vibra-
tions in the z and y direction.
• All bearings and the disc are placed in one node at a time.
• Simplified geometry, all chamfers, keys and small geometrical features are assumed to
have little significance.
• Constant angular velocity of the rotor system.
• Rigid disc, the disc does not deform and hence does not store potential energy.
• Potential energy associated with the up and down motion of the disc is assumed negli-
gibly.
• The disc rotation around y is assumed negligible.
• No stiffness is added to the system by attaching the disc.
• The bearings add stiffness and damping to the elements where they are attached.
B.1.2
Mathematical Model
The mathematical modelling of the mechanical system is split into three parts, the flexible
shaft, the rigid disc, and the bearings, respectively.
Shaft elements
The elements used for constructing the finite element model of the shaft is a 2-node ele-
ment that can move in the y and z direction and rotate around y and z The individual
time-dependent movement of a shaft element can be described by beam theory: xe(t) =
{x1(t), ...x8(t)}T. x1(t)...x4(t) describe the movement of the first node and x5(t)...x8(t) the
movement of the second node. x1(t) and x5(t) describes the linear motion in y, x2(t) and
x6(t) the linear motion in z, x3(t) and x7(t) the angular motion around y, and x4(t) and x8(t)
OMA Applied to Rotor-Bearing Systems via Pneumatic Actuation - Low Damping and Air
XI
==PAGE:108==
XII
B.1. The Flexible Rotor-Bearing System
the angular motion around z. The total potential energy related to bending deformation and
the total kinetic energy for angular and linear movement of the element can be described as
[16, eq. (26), (27)]:
Ee

2(xe)TKe
Bxe
(B.1)
Ee

Disc
As stated in the B.1.1 the disc is assumed rigid, and hence does not store potential energy,
hence can the equation of motion for the disc be defined as:
Md¨xd −ΩGd ˙xd = f d
(B.4)
where Md is the disc mass matrix for linear and angular motions and Gd is the gyroscopic
matrix for the disc.
Bearings
In the finite element method, bearings are represented by springs and dampers. The bearings
are assumed to be clamped so Mbb¨xbb = 0. The bearings are also assumed not to have influence
on the rotation of the shaft and this means that only translational movements are present.
This results in the equation of motion for the ball bearing being:
−Dbb ˙xbb −Kbbxbb = fbb
(B.5)
The ball bearing is assumed not to contribute to any damping of the system and hence
Dbb = 0.
The gas bearing model was developed by [3] by solving the modified Reynolds Equation, with
the injection velocity given by the Hagen-Poeseuille formula. There will not be elaborated
further on how this was done, and the reader is referred to [3] for more information. The gas
bearing introduces both stiffness and damping to the system; hence, the equations of motions
are therefore:
−Dgb ˙xgb −Kgbxgb = fgb
(B.6)
B.1.3
Global Model
The individual matrices are now combined into global mass, damping, and stiffness matrices,
with respect to the elements’ location in the model of the shaft. Since the model is linear,
the superposition principle can be used. The disc and bearings are placed directly to their
respective nodes. The global system can be written as:
MG¨xG + CG ˙xG + KGxG = fG
where:
CG = −ΩGG + DG
(B.7)

State Space Formulation

Discretisation
The shaft is discretised into 19 elements.
B.1.5
Prediction of Natural Frequencies, Damping Ratios and
Critical Speed
Using the discretisation of 19 elements, the natural frequencies, damping ratios, and mode
shapes can be predicted. This is done by solving the eigenvalue problem for the state space
formulation given in Equation B.8. From this, the eigenvalues and the eigenvectors of the
system are obtained. The imaginary part of the eigenvalues is the damped natural frequencies,
and the damping is found by the real part over the absolute part of the eigenvalues:
fd = ωd

2π
and

|λ|

Appendix B. Experimental Procedure
CXV
B.10
Improvement to the OMA Calculations
1
clear; clc; clf; close all; close all hidden;
2
format short g
3
addpath(genpath(fileparts(which(matlab.desktop.editor.getActiveFilename)))); %
Add that folder plus all subfolders to the path.
4
COLORS
5
Y = [1:30000;1:30000];
6
t = zeros(2,150,20);
7
create_data = false; % true if the old and new block hankel code has to be
runned
8
if create_data
9
for ii = 1:size(t,3)
10
for i = 1:size(t,2)
11
disp([ii,i])
12
s = i;
13
R = 2*s;
14
[nr, nc, nk] = size(Y);
15
% Old Construction of the Block Hankel
16
nk = nc;
17
tic
18
H = zeros(nr*R, nk-R+1);
19
for r=1:R
20
for c=1:nk-R+1
21
H(1+nr*(r-1):nr*r,c) = Y(:,r+c-1);
22
end
23
end
24
% New Construction of the Block Hankel
25
t(1,i,ii) = toc;
26
tic
27
idx = spdiags(triu(repmat(1:nk,R,1)));
28
idx = reshape(idx(:,1:nk-R+1) ,1,[]);
29
H1 = reshape(Y(:,idx),nr*R, nk-R+1);
30
SIZE(i) = prod(size(H1));
31
t(2,i,ii) = toc;
32
end
33
end
34
name = join(['C:\Users\madsf\OneDrive\Skrivebord\Speciale\Matlab\Data\
New_bhankel_time.txt']); % Creating save name
35
fopen(name);
36
t = reshape(pagetranspose(t),size(t,2) ,[]); % Creating the table
37
writematrix(t,name)
38
name = join(['C:\Users\madsf\OneDrive\Skrivebord\Speciale\Matlab\Data\
New_bhankel_size.txt']); % Creating save name
39
fopen(name);
40
writematrix(SIZE,name)
41
else
42
EXP = 'New_bhankel_time.txt'; % Creating the experiments name
43
t = load(EXP);
44
EXP = 'New_bhankel_size.txt'; % Creating the experiments name
45
SIZE = load(EXP);
46
end
47
48
T = t;
49
t = zeros(2,size(T,1));
50
t(1,:) = mean(T(:,1:2:end),2);
51
t(2,:) = mean(T(:,2:2:end),2);
==PAGE:212==
CXVI
B.10. Improvement to the OMA Calculations
52
53
disp(t)
54
disp(t(1,:)./t(2,:))
55
figure('Position', [200 200 1000 350])
56
plot(t(1,:)./t(2,:),'o-','linewidth',2,'markersize',3,'color',colors(1))
57
ylabel("Relation (Old Const. Time)/(New Const. Time)",'fontsize',11)
58
xlabel("s Block Rows",'fontsize',11)
59
sgtitle("Relation Between Old and New Block Hankel Consruction Time",'
fontsize',14)
60
grid on; grid minor
61
ax = gca;
62
ax.FontSize = 10;
63
saveas(gcf,"C:\Users\madsf\OneDrive\Skrivebord\Speciale\Figures\New_bHankel","
epsc")
